Here’s another way that leverage magnifies your returns and builds wealth faster than a cash purchase.

Over the years (if well selected), your investment properties will increase in price. If the price of that $100,000 property we’ve just discussed increases at an average annual rate of 3 percent, you earn another $3,000 a year. If its price increases at an annual rate of 5 percent, you’ll gain $5,000 more a year. And at a 7 percent annual rate of appreciation, your gains will hit $7,000 a year.

Add together annual net rental income and annual price gains and you’ll earn these total returns:

Total ROI = Income + Appreciation/Cash investment

Example 1: $100,000 all-cash purchase and (a) 3 percent, (b) 5 percent, and (c) 7 percent rates of appreciation:



(a) Total ROI = $10,000 + $3,000/$100,000 = 13%

(b) Total ROI = $10,000 + $5,000/$100,000 = 15%

(c) Total ROI = $10,000 + $7,000/$100,000 = 17%

Example 2: $50,000 down payment and (a) 3 percent, (b) 5 percent, and (c) 7 percent rates of appreciation:



(a) Total ROI = $5,596 + $3,000/$50,000 = 17.2%

(b) Total ROI = $5,596 + $5,000/$50,000 = 21.2%

(c) Total ROI = $5,596 + $7,000/$50,000 = 25.2%

Example 3: $25,000 down payment and (a) 3 percent, (b) 5 percent, and (c) 7 percent rates of appreciation:



(a) Total ROI = $3,394 + $3,000/$25,000 = 25.6%

(b) Total ROI = $3,394 + $5,000/$25,000 = 33.6%

(c) Total ROI = $3,394 + $7,000/$25,000 = 41.6%

Example 4: $10,000 down payment and (a) 3 percent, (b) 5 percent, and (c) 7 percent rates of appreciation.



(a) Total ROI = $2,073 + $3,000/$10,000 = 50.7%

(b) Total ROI = $2,073 + $5,000/$10,000 = 70.7%

(c) Total ROI = $2,073 + $7,000/$10,000 = 90.7%

When you total returns from annual net rental income and price increases, highly leveraged properties may produce almost unbelievably high annual rates of return—even without unrealistically high average annual rates of price increases. That’s why some investors in small rental properties have (over decades) built a net worth for themselves that runs into the millions of dollars. When you own rental properties, long-term rent and price increases grow acorns (relatively low down payments) into oak trees (property equity worth hundreds of thousands [or millions] of dollars).

As the years pass and you pay down your mortgage balances, a property portfolio of just 6 or 8 houses or perhaps 12 to 24 apartment units (more or less) can build enough wealth to guarantee a secure future. (Note, too, how potential investment property rates of return might easily exceed the widely believed [yet overstated] 10 percent average annual gains that the stock market has supposedly produced “over the long run.”)





Manage Your Risks


The advocates of get-rich-quick real estate schemes often fail to warn their followers that highly leveraged real estate magnifies risks as well as returns. Many naïve (as well as underfunded) real estate buyers have lost their shirts. These dreamers optimistically expected the market values of their properties to realize steady price gains of 10 to 20 percent a year—year after year just as steadily as the pages of their calendars are turned. They lost touch with reality. In fact, many investors barely cared what purchase prices they agreed to, what rent levels they collected, or how they financed their properties. They just knew that they could sell their properties within a few years for twice the amount they had paid.

One such investor bought a $300,000 fourplex with a down payment of $30,000. After paying property operating expenses and mortgage payments, the investor faced an alligator (negative cash flow) that chewed up $1,000 a month. But the investor figured that $1,000 a month was peanuts. He believed the future selling price of his property would go up 15 percent a year. Based on his expected rate of gain, here’s how this investor calculated the annual returns that he (unrealistically) hoped to receive:



As it turned out, this investor, like so many others, could not continue to feed his alligator $1,000 a month. He fell behind with his mortgage payments. As the market slowed, he was unable to sell (or refinance) the property. The lender foreclosed. The investor lost the property, his down payment of $30,000, the $24,000 of monthly negative cash shortfalls that he had covered prior to default, and his once-high credit score.

Here are four lessons you can take away from this investor’s regrettable (yet all too common and perfectly avoidable) losses:

Never expect the value of real estate, stocks, gold, antique automobiles, old masters, or any other type of investment to increase by 10, 15, or 20 percent year after year. When you need high rates of price gain to make your investment look attractive, you set yourself up for a big loss. The longer a bull market runs, the more likely it will exhaust itself (and perhaps suffer a near fatal heart attack).

Beware of negative cash flows. If the income you earn from an asset does not support the price you pay, you magnify your risk and chance of loss. You are speculating more than investing. That’s okay if that’s what you want to do. Just recognize that speculators (whether they realize it or not) play in a high-risk game that produces far more losers than winners. As negative cash flows increase, the probability of severe price correction looms large. Indeed, in his 1937 classic, The Theory of Investment Value (Harvard University Press), John Burr Williams wrote,





Every thoughtful investor knows that he should not confuse the real worth of an investment with its market price. No buyer considers all investments equally attractive. On the contrary, the wise investor seeks “the best at the price”. . . . Even then, he may not buy at all, for fear that everything is priced too high and nothing will give him his money’s worth.

If he does buy, he buys as an investor. He holds for income; if as a speculator for [quick] profit at sale. But over the long run, speculators must sell to investors [unless you believe that somehow speculative price increases can forever outrun people’s incomes and GDP] . . . therefore sooner or later, the ultimate values of all investments derive from a reasonable estimate of future income.



3. Beware of financial overreach. High leverage (a high loan-to-value ratio) usually requires large mortgage payments relative to the amount of net income that a property brings in. Even if at first you don’t suffer negative cash flows, vacancies, higher-than-expected expenses, or large rent concessions intended to attract good tenants can push you temporarily into the swamps where alligators feed. Over a period of, say, 10 to 20 years, owning real estate will grow your net worth many times over. But to enjoy that long-term future, you will likely pass through several cyclical downturns. Without cash (or credit) reserves to defend against alligator attacks, you may be pulled into the swamps and eaten alive before you find the safety and comfort of high ground.



4. Even when the property financing looks good, avoid overpaying for a property. Overly optimistic investors buy overpriced properties with little-or no-down-payment deals. In the earlier fourplex example, the investor agreed to pay $300,000 for the fourplex not because the property’s net income, that is, investment value, justified a price of $300,000. Rather, he paid $300,000 because he was excited about his easy-credit, low, 10 percent down payment financing. Compared with the $600,000 sales price that he expected to reap after just four or five years of ownership, his $300,000 purchase price looked cheap.



Again, I emphasize: I do not advise against low down payments (high leverage) per se. But when you do finance with high LTVs (loan to value), anticipate possible setbacks. To manage the risks of high-leverage OPM, adopt these six safeguards:

Buy bargain-priced properties. You build a financial cushion into your deals when you pay less than market value or pay less than a property is worth—which is not quite the same thing. Discover how in Chapters 4 through 7.

Buy properties that you can profitably improve. To build your net worth more quickly and reduce the risk of leverage, add value to your properties through creativity, sweat equity, remodeling, and renovation (see Chapter 8).

Buy properties with below-market rents that you can raise to market levels within a relatively short time (6 to 12 months). Increase your rental income and as a percentage of net operating income (NOI), your mortgage payments will become easier to pay. You will enjoy a larger margin of safety.

Buy properties with low-interest-rate financing such as mortgage assumptions, adjustable-rate mortgages, interest-rate buydowns, or seller financing. Low interest rates help you manage high debt. High interest rates make debt less affordable—especially beware of low interest rates that may disappear within just a few years (or less).

Buy right time, right price, right place properties. “All real estate is local” is an oft-cited cliché that contains a kernel of truth. Various neighborhoods and cities offer unique differences with respect to price, location, and market timing. To reduce risk and increase rewards, proactively study and select from such opportunities (see Chapter 8).

When high leverage presents an anxious level of risk, increase your down payment to lower your loan-to-value ratio and lower your monthly mortgage payments. If your available cash does not permit a higher down payment, bring in a money partner. Don’t act penny-wise and pound-foolish. Share gains with someone else rather than chance wrestling with a fierce alligator.





What Are Your Risk-Return Objectives?


Little- or nothing-down finance can increase your returns. Smart borrowing, when linked with smart investing, will grow acorns into oak trees. But the higher your LTVs (other things equal), the more you must prepare for a downturn in the rental market, unexpected property operating expenses, or a potential setback in your personal wherewithal. When you highly leverage your properties, a slight fall in rents may push you into negative cash flows. A decline in your property’s value can pull you underwater. Steer clear of sunshine-every-day optimism. Work through the investment numbers under several adverse scenarios. Decide what profits are worth pursuing and what risks you prefer to avoid.

If you can’t find properties in your favored area(s) that generate the numbers you would like to see, look elsewhere. Shift your discovery efforts from cities (or neighborhoods) that lack what you want to areas that offer more potential for the sources of returns that you find attractive (and doable). In fact, some international investors now believe that Detroit properties are attractive for their high incomes and low prices. They expect strong returns from cash yield, amortization, and price growth as the local economy gradually revitalizes and rebuilds.





MAXIMIZE LEVERAGE WITH OWNER-OCCUPANCY FINANCING


You now know the pros and cons of high leverage OPM (little or nothing down). Use it; do not abuse it. Among the best high-LTV choices for beginning investors: owner-occupied property financing.

The easiest-qualifying and often the lowest-cost way to borrow most (or even all) of the money you need to buy a property remains owner-occupied mortgage financing. Even with tight credit standards, high-LTV owner-occupied loan programs are readily available on single-family homes, condominiums, townhouses, and two- to four-unit apartment buildings. Owner occupancy allows LTVs as high as 90, 95, 97, or even 100 percent.

If you do not arrange for owner-occupied financing (that is, you choose not to live in the property), most lenders (banks, mortgage bankers, savings institutions) limit their mortgage loans on investment properties to 70 percent to 80 percent LTV ratio.3 Owner-occupied loans provide great advantage (as to LTV) relative to loans to finance purely rental properties. (However, some higher 85 to 95 percent investor loans are available, such as VA REOs—see Chapter 7.)

As another benefit, owner-occupants pay lower interest rates than investors. If lenders charge 4.0 to 5.0 percent for good-credit (say, FICO 720 or higher), owner-occupied loans, the interest rate for investor properties will probably range between 4.75 and 5.25 percent. As a beginning real estate investor, weigh the possibilities of owner-occupied mortgage loans. [Note: The spread between owner occupant interest rates and those rates available to investors does not remain constant. As lenders perceive greater loan risk, the spread widens. When blue sky forecasts prevail, interest rate (and LTV) spreads narrow. When len ders feel optimistic, the costs and terms for investor loans nearly match those available to owner occupants.]





Owner-Occupied Buying Strategies


To begin investing in rental properties with little or nothing down, select a high-LTV owner-occupant loan program that appeals to you.4 Buy a one- to four-unit property. Live in it for one year, then rent it out, and repeat the process. After you obtain owner-occupied financing, that loan can remain on the property even after you move out. Because the second, third, and even fourth homes you buy and occupy will still qualify for high-LTV financing (low or nothing down), you can—within a period of five or six years—accumulate as many as 8 to 16 rental units in addition to your own residence—all without large down payments.

You can work this homeowner gambit two, three, maybe four times. But you cannot pursue it indefinitely. At some point, lenders may shut you off from owner-occupied financing because they will object to your game plan. Also, some lenders will not count all of your rent collections as part of your qualifying ratios. In such cases, you will need to earn a large enough salary to continue your ability to qualify for new high-LTV mortgages. Nevertheless, serial owner-occupancy acquisitions can surely help you acquire (at least) your first two or three investment properties.





Current Homeowners, Too, Can Use This Method


Even if you own your own home, weigh the advantages of owner-occupied financing to acquire your next several investment properties. Here’s how: Locate a property (condo, house, two- to four-unit apartment building) that you can buy and move in to. Find a good tenant for your current house or condo. Complete the financing on your new property and move in to it. If you like your current home, at the end of one year, rent out your most recently acquired property and move back into your former residence. Alternatively, find another “home” to buy and again finance this property with a new owner-occupied, high-leverage (LTV), low-rate mortgage.





Why One Year?


To qualify for owner-occupied financing, you are required to assure the lender that you intend to live in the house for at least one year. Intend, however, does not mean guarantee. You can (for good reason or no reason) change your mind. Beware: Do not lie about your intent to occupy a property. Lying to obtain a loan violates state and federal laws. Governments do prosecute for this offense.

During the past property boom, borrowers lied and loan reps winked. Many borrowers and loan reps brazenly failed to comply with the intent-to-occupy rule. In fact, a loan rep at Wachovia (a bank that subsequently went bust) suggested that I finance a new investment property that I was buying (out of state) as if it were a second home. I declined.

To succeed in real estate over the short and long term, establish, maintain, and nurture your credibility with lenders—and everyone else with whom you want to enter transactions or build a relationship of trust. To slip through loopholes, make false promises, sidestep agreements, or engage in other sleights will destroy your reputation for integrity. Unless you encounter an unexpected turn of events, honor a lender’s one-year occupancy requirement.





Where Can You Find High-LTV Owner-Occupied Mortgages?


Everywhere! Even in today’s tighter credit market, walk your fingers through the Yellow Pages to “mortgages.” Then call banks, savings institutions, mortgage bankers, mortgage brokers, and credit unions. Mortgage lenders advertise in local daily newspapers and on the Internet. Check with your state, county, or city departments of housing finance. Many homebuilders and Realtors know of various types of low- or nothing-down home finance programs. Explore the websites hud.gov, va.gov, homesteps.com (Freddie Mac), and fanniemae.com.





WHAT ARE THE LOAN LIMITS?


The specific maximum amount you can borrow under these high-LTV programs varies by type of loan and area of the country. However, the loan limits are high enough to finance properties in good neighborhoods. For example, Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae programs nationwide will lend up to the following amounts (usually adjusted upward each year):

Number of Units Contiguous States, District of Columbia, and Puerto Rico Higher-Priced Areas

1 $417,000 $625,000

2 $533,850 $800,775

3 $645,300 $967,950

4 $801,950 $1,202,925



FHA varies its loan limits by county. Here are several examples:

Low-Cost Areas (FHA/HUD)

Type of Residence Loan Limits

One-unit $271,050

Two-unit 347,000

Three-unit 419,425

Four-unit 521,250

High-Cost Areas (FHA/HUD)

Type of Residence Loan Limits

One-unit $729,750

Two-unit 934,200

Three-unit 1,129,350

Four-unit 1,403,400



For VA loans, eligible veterans may originate—and veterans and nonveterans alike may assume—up to the following amounts:5

Lowest-Price Areas Highest-Price Areas

$417,000 $625,000



Unlike Fannie, Freddie, and the FHA, the VA applies the same loan limit for one- to four-unit properties. The VA, however, has boosted its loan limit and, like the FHA, varies each specific limit according to the prevailing local price levels. One- to four-unit properties that are priced higher than these stated limits seldom bring in rents high enough to cover expenses and mortgage payments. When you select rentals that pay for themselves—even with a small down payment—the limits set by FHA and VA will not unduly limit your choice of properties. (FHA and VA adjust their loan limits periodically.)





HIGH LEVERAGE FOR INVESTOR-OWNER FINANCING


Say that you’ve maxed out your high-LTV (low down payment) owner-occupancy financing possibilities. Or maybe you’re happy in your present home. No way will you (or your spouse) move (even temporarily) to another property. Without owner-occupied, what other ways help you avoid placing 20 to 30 percent of the purchase price in cash from your own savings? What high leverage (low down payment) OPM (other people’s money) techniques can you use to buy and finance your investment properties?





High Leverage versus Low (or No) Down Payment


Note this paradox: High leverage does not necessarily imply a low down payment. High leverage means that you’ve acquired a property using little cash (say, 10 to 20 percent of the purchase price, or sometimes less) from your own funds.

You find a property priced at $400,000, and your lender agrees to provide a first mortgage in the amount of $280,000. Because you can’t (or don’t want to) draw $120,000 from your savings to make this large down payment, you can find another way to raise all (or part) of the cash required. When successful, you will have achieved a highly leveraged transaction. You control a $400,000 property with relatively little money pulled from your own pocket.

You can gain the benefits (and risks) of high leverage in two ways: (1) originate (or assume) a high-LTV first mortgage, or (2) originate (or assume) a lower-LTV mortgage. Then to reduce (or eliminate) out-of-pocket cash, use other sources (loans, equity partners) to fill in some (or all) of the difference between the amount of the first mortgage and the purchase price of the property.





Creative Finance Revisited


Creative finance encourages you to think through multiple financing alternatives. The term gained popularity when property prices shot up and millions of hopeful buyers felt shut out of the fast and seemingly easy money because they lacked sufficient cash, credit, or both to enter the game. “The central theme of my course,” wrote Ed Beckley in No Down Payment Formula, “is to teach you how to acquire as much property as you can without using any of your own money. . . . Starting from scratch requires that you become extremely resourceful. You need to substitute ideas for cash” (p. 69).

How might investors use creative thinking and resourcefulness to substitute for cash?

Look for a More Liberal Lender. Under today’s tighter underwriting standards, most banks and savings institutions loan only 70 to 80 percent of an (non-owner-occupied) income property’s appraised value. However, some specialty lenders will make 90 percent (or higher) LTV loans. When credit markets ease, lender competition will most likely (once again) edge up investor LTVs.

Also, some wealthy private investors provide high-LTV mortgages. Find these private investors through newspaper classified ads and websites: Advertise in the “Capital Wanted” section, or telephone those who list themselves in the “Capital Available” section. Also call mortgage brokers who may have contacts with as many as 20 to 100 sources of property financing. Through their lengthy list of lenders, mortgage brokers may find you a high LTV.

Beware: Read the fine print of the loan agreement. Many of these nontraditional (subprime, hard money) mortgages include costly up-front fees, high interest, and steep prepayment penalties. Foolish optimism plus subprime (or unconventional) loan equals foreclosure.





Second Mortgages


Before the credit crisis, some investment property lenders permitted what are called 70–20–10 loans, or other variations such as 75–15–10, or maybe even 80–15–5. The first figure refers to the LTV of a first mortgage; the second figure refers to the percentage of the purchase price represented by a second mortgage (often owner financing); and the third figure refers to out-of-pocket cash contributed by the buyer (or equity partners). A 70–20–10 deal for the purchase of a $100,000 property would require the following amounts:

First mortgage $70,000

Second mortgage $20,000

Buyer’s cash $10,000



The property seller makes the best source for second mortgage loans. Referred to as seller seconds or seller carrybacks, these loans require less red tape, paperwork, and closing costs. You can sometimes persuade a seller to accept an interest rate that’s lower than what a commercial lender would charge. At a time when first mortgage rates were at 11 percent, and second mortgage rates were at 16 percent, one of my sellers carried back a $75,000 interest-only mortgage loan at a rate of 8 percent.6 (Sellers are more likely to offer below-market interest rates when market interest rates climb upward—unlike today’s low market rates.) My deal looked like this:

Purchase price $319,500

First mortgage at 11% $180,000

Seller second at 8% $75,000

Cash from buyer $64,500



If the sellers won’t cooperate, look to private investors, mortgage brokers, banks, and savings institutions. Before you do turn to loan-sharking commercial second mortgage lenders, think of friends or family members who might like to earn a relatively safe return of 6 to 8 percent (more or less) on their money. Compared to certificates of deposit and passbook savings that in most times pay interest between 2 and 5 percent, a 6 to 8 percent rate of interest could look pretty good (and especially good with today’s CD rates of 2 percent or less).





Borrow against Other Assets


If you’re a homeowner with good credit, arrange a home equity loan (that is, second mortgage) on your home. Through an equity line of credit you might raise cash for a down payment—but prepare for adverse changes in the market or your own finances. Home equity loans are often written with variable interest rates. Low interest today, but potentially higher rates tomorrow. Alternatively, consider a high-LTV, cash-out refinance of your home with a newly originated first mortgage.

What other assets can you borrow against? Retirement accounts, cars, jewelry, artwork, coin collection, life insurance, or vacation home? List everything you own. You may surprise yourself at what you discover. On the other hand, if you fail to discover other assets, reprioritize your spending and saving (see further on).





Convert Assets to Cash (Downsizing)


Rather than borrow against your assets, downsize. Friends of mine sold their 6,000-square-foot Chicago North Shore residence for $1,050,000. With those proceeds they bought a vacation home, a smaller primary residence, and an in-town condominium. In the United Kingdom, they call this popular practice mouse-holing.

Could you live comfortably in a smaller or less expensive home? Are you wasting money on unproductive luxury cars, jewelry, watches, or clothing? Are you one of the many Americans who live the high life—for now—but fail to build enough net worth to support the twin goals of financial security and financial independence?

In their study of the affluent, Thomas Stanley and William Danko (The Millionaire Next Door) interviewed a sample of high-income professionals whom the authors named UAWs (under-accumulators of wealth). They also chose a sample of PAWs (prodigious accumulators of wealth)—who worked in less prestigious jobs and earned less income. Because of wise budgeting and investing, the net worth of PAW households far surpassed the net worth of those higher-income, yet profligate spenders. Downsizing now rewards with high payback later. (In keeping with the theme of frugal living and smart investing, Stanley titled his latest book Stop Living Rich.)





Wraparound Mortgage


A wraparound mortgage helps buyers obtain a high LTV while it provides a seller with a good yield. Investors use wraparounds to gain some benefit from a below-current-market-rate loan that exists against a property—and yet also obtain a relatively high LTV.

To accomplish these twin goals, the investor wraps the existing below-market loan with a new wraparound loan at a higher interest rate. The seller continues to pay the existing low interest rate loan while the buyer pays the seller on the new wraparound loan. The seller profits on the spread in interest rates. The wraparound works best during periods when interest rates shoot up. Figure 2.1 provides an example.



Figure 2.1 Wraparound Loan Example





In Figure 2.1, the seller creates and carries a new loan of $210,000 at 9.0 percent. Payments on the seller’s existing first mortgage total $1,077 per month. Payments on the new wraparound loan are $1,691 per month. Therefore, the seller earns a $614 per month spread. As a return on the $60,000 equity the seller has left in the deal, he earns a return of 12.28 percent (12 × 614 ÷ 60,000 = 12.28). The buyer-investor gains because the interest rate charged him by the seller falls below the then-prevailing market interest rate on newly originated loans.

If, as a buyer, you wrap a so-called nonassumable mortgage, the seller’s mortgage lender may choose to exercise its due-on-sale clause. If that happens, the sellers (or you) would have to pay off the seller’s mortgage and arrange new financing. However, during the past 25 years—and especially during periods of high foreclosures, lenders rarely throw their performing loans into default by demanding full payment because a seller breached a technical requirement of the loan agreement.





Use Credit Cards


Although inviting disaster, some investors take advantage of every credit card offer they receive in the mail. Then when they require quick cash to close a deal, they raise $10,000, $25,000, or even $50,000 from cash advances. Investors sometimes even pay cash for a property with the entire sum raised from credit cards.

It makes no sense to use credit card cash advances for long-term financing. Yet, on occasion, plastic might serve as a quick and convenient source to cover short-term needs. You might find a steal of a deal that you can buy (and renovate?), then immediately flip (resell) the property at a profit. Or maybe you need quick money to place great property under contract.

For my taste, credit card cash costs too much and exposes the borrower to excessive risk. But it remains a possibility if you realistically calculate that the potential rewards decisively trump the risks—and you see multiple unlocked exit doors.





Personal Loans


In the days before credit card cash advances (which are now the most popular type of personal loan), personal loans were called signature loans. As you build your net worth, lenders will grant you signature loans—at much lower fees and interest rates than credit card cash advances—in amounts of $10,000, $25,000, $50,000, or even $250,000, if your credit record and net worth can support repayment. Use the money from these signature loans for down payments or renovation funds.

Although mortgage lenders do not favor borrowers who use personal loans for down payments, investors routinely find ways to do so. OWC (owner will carry) sellers who finance property sales probably won’t inquire (and maybe won’t care) about the source of your down payment. If you’re short on cash, compare the risks and benefits of using a cash advance or signature loan to raise money for your down payment.

But again, count the risk factors. Ill-conceived borrowing can push you into those ugly toss-and-turn, sleepless nights, if not financial ruin. Make sure repayment does not depend on some speculative (uncertain or unpredictable) contingency.





Land Contracts


A land contract—sometimes referred to as a contract for deed, contract of sale, or agreement of sale—permits buyers to pay sellers for their property in installments. The term land contract became popular because this type of financing was often used to buy acreage (especially farmland).

Under a land contract, a buyer agrees to purchase a property and pay principal and interest to the seller. Property title remains in the seller’s name until conditions of the contract are fulfilled. The buyer takes possession of the property. If a buyer defaults on the agreement, the seller can typically repossess (not foreclose on) the property. Sellers favor repossessions because they’re typically quicker, easier, and less costly than foreclosure. It’s even common for buyers who default on land contracts to voluntarily return the collateral property to the seller. In this way, the buyer avoids the negative mark on his credit record that would otherwise result from a seller’s suit for recovery of the property.





Verify State Law


Consumer protection laws in some states now entangle the property repossession process with more cumbersome requirements. As a buyer, you might welcome such protection. Yet, if entanglement endangers the simplicity of the agreement, sellers may choose to avoid offering OWC.

Contract for deeds may become useful when wrapping low interest rate financing (see the previous discussion of wraparounds). Caution: These contracts include pitfalls for buyers and sellers. Because evolving state law governs land contract sales, consult a competent, local real estate attorney before entering into this form of financing (either as buyer or seller.).

Often, properties financed with land contracts are nonconforming properties such as storefronts with living quarters in the rear or above, farmhouses with acreage, older properties in need of repair, or a large house that has been split up into multiple apartment units. My first investment was a five-unit property—a large, old house split into four one-bedroom apartments and a one-bedroom, one-bath carriage house that sat on the rear of the lot.

I paid $10,000 down, and the seller (an elderly widow) financed the $90,000 balance at 5 percent interest. She said that interest rate matched what she could receive from a bank and she didn’t think it ethical to charge me a higher rate. That experience occurred decades ago. But, amazingly, a 5 percent interest rate might again look attractive to sellers today—as it far exceeds the bank CD rates now available.

In the present tight-credit market, a contract for deed serves the same purposes they always have (nonconforming properties or subpar cash or credit buyers). During the credit and property boom, land contracts diminished in use because mortgage lenders opened their vaults for almost anyone who applied for a loan. With lenders now tight-fisted, contract for deeds are making a comeback. And they are not limited to buyers who lack cash or credit. Even some creditworthy buyers might accept land contract financing to avoid the piled-higher-and-deeper paperwork required by banks.

In addition to land contracts, two types of easy credit, low-down-payment, seller-assisted financing have become widespread: lease-purchase and lease-option agreements (see Chapter 9). To some degree, many sellers prefer these rent-to-own alternatives because (if written properly), they eliminate the need for court action to recover a property—except perhaps the rental eviction process—if a defaulting lease-option buyer refuses to voluntarily vacate. Nevertheless, land contracts remain available to play a role in your property acquisition strategy (and later perhaps, in your disposition of properties).

Properly written, land contracts are low cost and relatively easy to complete; they offer flexibility in price, terms, and interest rate; and they can provide sellers quick remedies for borrower default while they protect buyers against unfair forfeiture and future title problems. If you find (or create) a good deal using a land contract, don’t pass it up without thoughtful review (and, certainly, informed legal advice).





Sweat Equity (Create Value through Renovations)


You might buy some properties with 100 percent financing if you can enhance their value through improvements and renovations. Say you find a property that should sell (in tip-top condition) for around $250,000. Yet, because of its sorry state of repair, as well as an eager seller, you can buy the property at a price of $170,000 with short-term, no-money-down owner financing (maybe a lease option or lease purchase). You then contribute labor (sweat) and $17,500 in materials that you pay for with credit cards.

After you complete your work and bring the property up to its $250,000 market value, you arrange a 75 percent LTV mortgage with a lender. With your loan proceeds of $187,500 (.75 × $250,000), you pay off the seller and your credit card account. Voilà—you have not only achieved 100 percent financing for your acquisition price and rehab but also created $67,500 of instant equity.





More OPM Techniques


In addition to the high leverage techniques discussed, you might choose from the following OPM possibilities:

Bring in partners. If you can’t (or don’t want to) draw on your own cash or borrowing power, find a good deal and then explain to potential money partners (friends, family, contractors, wealthy investors) how they will benefit if they join you in the transaction.

Agree to swap services or products. Would the seller accept some service or expertise that you can provide? Take stock of your skills (law, medicine, dentistry, writing, advertising, carpentry, accounting, landscaping, architecture, and so on). How about products? Say you own a radio station or newspaper. Trade off advertising time or space for a down payment. Anything you can produce, deliver, or sell wholesale (at cost) might work.

Borrow (or reduce) the real estate commission. Most brokers and sales agents hate this technique. Still, sometimes buyers and sellers do ask the agents involved in a transaction to defer payment until some later date. A few agents like to leave their commissions invested in a property. In doing so, they avoid (or defer) paying income taxes on their commission. At the same time, they earn interest or receive a piece of the action (equity in the property and future profits from a sale).

Simultaneously sell (or lease) part of the property. Does the property include an extra lot, mobile home, or perhaps timber, oil, gas, air, or mineral rights that you could sell? Yes . . . then sell, lease, or license these rights simultaneously with your acquisition. These proceeds will help you fund your transaction.

Prepaid rents and tenant security deposits. Buy an investment property and you are entitled to the existing tenants’ security deposits and prepaid rents. Say you close on June 2. The seller (of a fourplex) is holding $4,000 in tenant security deposits and $3,800 in rent money that applies to the remaining days in June. Together, the deposits and prepaid rents amount to $7,800. In most transactions, these monies reduce your cash-to-close.

Create paper. You’ve asked the seller to accept owner financing with 10 percent down. She balks. She believes the deal places her at risk. Alleviate her fears and bolster her security. Offer her a lien against your car or a second (or third) mortgage on another property you own. Write into the loan agreement that when principal payments and property appreciation (added together) lift your equity to 20 percent (LTV of 80 percent), the seller will remove the security lien she holds against your other pledged asset(s).

Lease/purchase options. Because of their popularity, a more extensive discussion in Chapter 9 surveys the pros and cons of lease or purchase options.





Are High-Leverage Creative-Finance Purchases Readily Available?


I have used some type of creative finance (as either buyer or seller) in more than one-half of the property transactions in which I’ve been involved. To make creative finance work, however, know what you are doing. Envision ways the deal might turn against you. Calm your eagerness to make the deal. Concede, but also request quid pro quo.

Become Wary: Low-down, easy-money transactions tempt new investors into buying troubled properties or paying excessive prices. Honor commitments—but commit slowly and thoughtfully. Are you convinced that you will always want to fulfill this commitment? What contingencies might change your mind?





Fewer Sellers, Fewer Properties


Because creative finance bypasses conventional norms, your property purchase choices become fewer. Though nearly every seller will accept cash, a much smaller number will provide financing with little or nothing down. Nevertheless, such transactions not only occur, they sometimes occur unexpectedly. Sellers who first claim they’re not interested will soften their attitude once you persuasively explain what’s in it for them and then tender your signed offer.





Lower-Quality Overpriced Lemons


Beware of property owners who want to entice you into buying overpriced lemons. These sellers have read the nothing-down books. They know that advertising come-ons like “owner financing,” “nothing down,” “no qualifying,” “make offer,” and “EZ terms” will bring dozens of calls from the latest “graduates” of the Ima Guru School of Creative Finance and Credit Repair.

Standing ready to fleece these sheep, savvy sellers surreptitiously lead eager buyers into ill-advised purchase contracts. Maintenance and repair problems, disappearing tenants, phantom leases, illegal units, neighborhood crime, short-term balloon mortgages, troublesome neighbors, and undisclosed liens represent just a few of the setbacks naïve buyers have run in to. [For many other types of potential problems, see my book The 106 Common Mistakes Homebuyers Make (and How to Avoid Them), 4th ed. (John Wiley & Sons, 2008.)] In other words, don’t let the siren call of easy finance crash your boat onto the rocks before you’ve barely left port.





BUILD CONFIDENCE WITH LENDERS, INVESTORS, SELLERS, AND SALES AGENTS


How can you effectively raise debt or equity capital, or both (OPM) to convince potential lenders and partners that they can place their confidence in you? How do you instill their confidence? Prepare and present yourself to satisfactorily meet the 7 Cs (criteria of credibility) for raising money and attracting profitable opportunities.7

Here’s what I mean: Evaluate this “call for help” posting from “Ralph” that I recently saw on a real estate website:

Hi! I’m a new investor. . . . I want to concentrate on rehabbing and flip.

As my credit score is 580, and my wife is back in school (no savings, and so on) . . . I’m having trouble finding 100 percent funding for that first deal. I’ve found several houses which I think would work well for fix and flip, but they slipped away before I could find the funding. . . . I keep hearing about building a small cash reserve by bird-dogging for others, but if I can come up with a 100 percent hard money loan here in the Denver area, and the numbers work for a high interest rate, back end points, and so on . . . I would prefer to jump right in.



Would you provide a mortgage loan to this newbie? Would you invest equity money in one of his deals? If you were a real estate agent, would Ralph make it onto your list of favored clients (that list of investors you alert first when a too-good-to-last new listing comes onto the market)?

Not likely, right? Nothing in this would-be investor’s résumé instills confidence that he could carry out his fix and flip successfully. Look at Ralph’s personal facts: low credit score, no experience or track record, no savings, and an almost desperate plea for money—he’s even willing to accept whatever loan terms a hard money lender might require. And although he does not specifically admit it here, I suspect that all of Ralph’s current income goes out to pay bills and living expenses—as fast as (or maybe faster than) it comes in.

With such risk factors piled up against him, he still wants to “jump right in.” Ralph evidently sees no need to prepare. He seems to believe that real estate will remedy his precarious financial condition—if he could just find someone who will lend him the money he wants. Ralph represents a superb example of the “no cash, no credit, no experience” mindset. Ralph fails to understand the lesson that I conveyed at the beginning of this chapter. Rarely can property help anyone solve one’s “no cash, poor credit” money troubles.

In fact, I would bet that if a hard money lender (or any lender, for that matter) did provide enough money for Ralph to acquire a fixer-upper, he soon would find himself in a sorrier fiscal condition than his current situation poses. As every experienced renovator has learned, most fix-and-sell projects take more time and more money than the original budget estimates. Cost overruns, contractor delays, unforeseen problems that show up after you have begun the work, mistakes in sequencing the work, and a slower-than-hoped-for resale can plague even the wisest of the fix and flippers.

If you think that my comments here are meant to discourage “jumping into real estate,” you are right. I want you (and Ralph) to succeed in real estate . . . not merely invest per se. Before you jump in, use the 7 Cs of credibility to prepare your resume. Strengthen each of the 7 Cs. Capital and opportunities will seek you out.

The 7 Cs refer to the loan underwriting standards that lenders apply when they evaluate potential borrowers. But you also need to establish credibility with equity investors, property sellers, and real estate agents. No one in real estate wants to waste time with the unprepared and unqualified. For each of the following Cs, rate yourself. Then list and execute the steps that you will take to strengthen each one.

Credit

Capacity

Cash reserves

Collateral

Character and personal characteristics

Competency and experience

Compensating factors





Credit Scores and Credit Record


If your credit record shows imperfections (or worse), you can still qualify for financing. Owner will carry (OWC), lease options, lease purchase, co-signers, FHA, VA, some state housing programs, and subject-to properties all represent possibilities. But there’s no getting around the fact that if (in today’s market) your credit score falls below 720 or so, you will face tougher qualifying paperwork, and more than likely, pay an interest rate that exceeds the lowest rate available. To build credibility and confidence, manage your credit to enhance your credit score (at least to achieve a responsible number).

Good credit increases your possibilities. Without a strong credit score, you’ll limit yourself to government programs, seller financing (most likely with above-market price or terms), subject-to loan or purchase agreements, or subprime B, C, or D loans that carry higher discount points, origination fees, and interest rates (if such loans are even available). When you show an excellent credit score and credit record, lenders will welcome you with competitive rates and terms. You become a sought-after customer for lenders, sales agents, and sellers.

Nevertheless, some mortgage lenders will accept borrowers who have experienced foreclosure, repossession, or bankruptcy. To qualify with these lenders, you typically need to (1) have paid on time, every time, for the past 18 to 24 months; (2) attribute previous adverse credit to divorce, unemployment, accident, illness, or other outside-your-control misfortune; and (3) convince the lender that your present and future financial well-being stands on a concrete foundation. (To improve your credit score, see myfico.com.)





Capacity (Monthly Income)


To judge your capacity to pay, mortgage lenders evaluate your monthly income from employment and other sources, as well as the expected rents and NOI of the financed property. For owner-occupied properties, a lender will (directly or indirectly) emphasize qualifying ratios. A qualifying ratio is the percentage of your income that you can safely allocate to mortgage payments (principal, interest, property taxes, and insurance, or PITI). If a lender sets a 28 percent housing-cost qualifying ratio and you gross $4,000 a month, the lender may limit your mortgage payments (PITI) to $1,120 a month.

If you are buying your first or second rental house, the lender may count 75 percent of the rents toward your qualifying ratios—but on this point lenders differ. Some will count more and some will count less. When financing your first few investment properties, lenders rely most on your personal credit and income. As you gain investment experience, they become more willing to look at your individual properties on a stand-alone basis. In times of strict underwriting, credit counts as much or more than capacity—even for investment-sized properties.

To judge capacity for apartment buildings and commercial properties, lenders apply a debt coverage ratio (DCR). A debt coverage ratio shows the lender whether the property brings in enough rental income to cover operating expenses and debt service (principal and interest). Here’s an example of DCR for a fourplex whose units each rent for $750 a month:

Gross potential income (4 × $750 × 12) minus $36,000

Vacancy and collection losses (6% of GPI). $2,160

Operating expenses and upkeep $7,200

Property taxes and insurance equals $2,360

Net operating income (NOI) $24,280



If a lender sets a 25 percent margin of net income over debt service, divide the property’s annual NOI by a debt coverage ratio (DCR) of 1.25:



To check, we reverse the calculations:



From these figures, you can see that with a required 1.25 DCR, the property will yield enough income to support a monthly mortgage payment of $1,619 ($19,424 annual mortgage payment ÷ 12). To figure how much loan a mortgage payment of $1,619 a month will pay off (amortize) over a 30-year term, refer to Table 2.1.

Table 2.1 Monthly Payment Required per $1,000 of Original Mortgage Balance



At mortgage interest rates of, say, 6.0, 7.5, or 9.0 percent, the lender could lend you up to $270,284, $231,617, or $201,118, respectively.

6.0% Mortgage Interest Rate

$1,619/$5.99 = $270,284 loan amount

7.5% Mortgage Interest Rate

$1,619/$6.99 = $231,617 loan amount

9% Mortgage Interest Rate

$1,619/$8.05 = $201,118 loan amount

DCRs represent guidelines, not absolutes. Given the strength (or weakness) of your other Cs, lenders will adjust their desired DCR either higher or lower. The quality of the property’s income stream will also count. A property leased to a AAA-rated corporate tenant (or perhaps even one rented to long-term Section 8 tenants) will deserve a lower DCR than a property that produces less stable rent collections—such as a small strip shopping center with frequent vacancies and an extensive turnover of its small business tenants.





Cash Reserves and Source of Down Payment


Your lender sets a 70 percent LTV for its first mortgage. Your seller will carry back a second mortgage for the balance of the purchase price. Will the lender approve your loan application? Maybe, maybe not. (Today, probably not—but expect an easing up when normal underwriting returns.)

Regardless of LTV, lenders like to see their borrowers invest their own cash into their properties. The lender will also want to know where you’re getting the cash. The best source is ready money from a savings account (or other liquid assets). More than likely, the lender will require a seasoned, proof-of-funds bank statement. Most lenders would not like to learn that you’re taking a $10,000 cash advance against your credit card.

Likewise, lenders want you to retain cash (or liquid assets) after you close your loan. Often, they like to see enough reserves to cover two or three months of mortgage payments. However, the larger the cash balances that you maintain on hand, the better you look. Just as important, the larger your cash balances, the less likely a storm will capsize your investment plans.





Collateral


Lenders want to be repaid. If you default on your loan obligations, the lender wants an asset (or assets) to seize. They want collateral.

Until you move up to larger multifamily or commercial-size properties (at least several million dollars or more), lenders will rate your personal Cs more than they will consider the property itself (hard money lenders excepted). Nevertheless, lenders do set standards for properties (not just borrowers).

Though their standards and practices vary, some lenders won’t finance properties larger than four units (or even two units). Some won’t finance properties in poor condition or those located in deteriorating neighborhoods or states hit hard by foreclosures. Many lenders verify that a property is serviced by all utilities (for example, some lenders avoid properties with septic tanks instead of sewer lines). Lenders set standards that apply to property age; construction materials; type of roof; central heat and air; electrical system; paved streets; conformance with zoning and building regulations; convenience to schools, public transportation, shopping, and job centers; land/building ratio; and other criteria.

Before you look at properties and write up an offer to buy, verify whether that property meets the criteria of the lender and loan program that you intend to use. Otherwise, you may waste time and money (loan application costs, appraisal, and other miscellaneous fees). Often, lenders will not refund these prepaid expenses.





Loan-to-Value Ratios


As we talked about earlier, lenders lend up to a specified percentage of a property’s market value (or contract price—whichever is less). After your property meets the lender’s features and location profile, the lender will arrange for an independent estimate of market value, which, as Chapter 3 explains, may or may not prove accurate or reasonable.

In boom times, appraisers tend to overappraise—which is one reason why so many borrowers are now underwater and lenders have pleaded for government to rescue them from the effects of their ill-conceived and reckless lending practices. During the boom, rarely did any appraisal come in with a low, deal-killing valuation. Whatever amount the lender needed to support the loan, that’s the amount the appraiser delivered. As the old joke goes, Member of the Appraisal Institute (MAI) stood for Made As Instructed. Perhaps unfair, but there’s no doubt that appraisers felt pressured to hit the numbers (market value estimate) that would please the parties to a transaction.

In tough markets as exist now, appraisers are more likely to underappraise. They feel pressure from government regulators. New federal rules prohibit lenders from directly ordering appraisals. Loan reps must submit their appraisal requests to a supposedly impartial appraisal management company. That company then chooses the appraiser to assign to the subject property.

Complaints now arise that the assigned appraisers don’t know the territory. If their value estimate comes in too low to support the requested LTV, the borrower, lender, and/or seller will claim that the comps are not really similar to the subject property. The subject property’s location (or features) stand far superior to the comparables that the appraiser flagrantly ignores.

Thus, as an investor, you face two potential pitfalls: (1) in times of easy money lending, the lender may approve a loan that exceeds the safe amount you should borrow (without accepting undue risk); and (2) in tough times, the appraisal may come up short. It will either kill your deal or force you to increase the amount of your down payment. To avoid any or all of these underwriting issues, carefully evaluate the lender’s appraisal (see Chapter 3).





Recourse to Other Assets or Income


In addition to the subject property, lenders might also require you to pledge other assets (property, bank accounts, stocks, and so on) to strengthen their security. At times, you might offer cross-collateralization (or pledged collateral) to a bank in exchange for an LTV that’s higher than you might otherwise receive. In close calls (and sometimes not so close), a boost in the collateral you provide will tip loan approval in your direction.

Besides collateral, banks (and sellers) will probably ask property investors to sign a personal note to further guarantee repayment of the monies owed. If you default, a recourse loan gives the lender (note holder) the right to go after not just the mortgaged property or other pledged assets, but also, more generally, other assets in which you own an interest or income you earn or receive. Check your state law for the recourse rights that mortgage lenders typically place in their loan agreements—and how (whether) courts enforce these lenders’ rights.

Because during the boom, lenders easily granted nonrecourse property loans, a New York City private equity firm recently walked away from a $5.5 billion property (now worth maybe $2.6 billion) and left the banks that had financed the deal with a loss of more than $2.4 billion. With loss experience like that, you can see why lenders have become gun-shy about offering nonrecourse mortgages.

Will times change? Will nonrecourse again become standard operating procedure on mega-million and billion-dollar deals? Probably. Monitor the credit cycle. Liberal lending will come back (if, as they say, history repeats itself).





Character


U.S. lenders cannot legally exclude borrowers because of their age, race, religion, sex, marital status, or disability. They can and do look at character and personal characteristics such as the following:

Education level

Career advancement potential

Job stability

Stability in the community

Saving, spending, and borrowing habits

Dependability and consistency

Dress and mannerisms



A mortgage lender will not arbitrarily reject your loan application because you dropped out of high school, dyed your hair purple, tattooed every piece of flesh on your body, wear a silver nose ring, change jobs every six months, or have not registered a telephone in your own name. Nevertheless, subtle (and not so subtle) influences still count—especially for investors, and especially when tight underwriting prevails.

Property investment requires commitment. Smart lenders trust you to fulfill your responsibilities. Convince the lender that you are a solid and dependable worker, investor, and borrower. The legendary banker J. P. Morgan once testified to a U.S. congressional committee, “Money cannot buy credit. A man I do not trust could not get credit from me on all the bonds of Christendom.” Character does count. Not merely with lenders, but even more so with potential partners and equity investors.

As liars’ loans proliferated from 2001 to 2006, many lenders ignored (to their later regret) J. P. Morgan’s wise advice. And more than likely, during some future boom period, lenders will again lose their senses and overlook loan application mistakes and exaggerations. Yet, in all of your personal and business transactions, resist temptation. Become known as someone whose words, actions, and promises evoke trust and confidence.





Competence and Experience


Borrowers default on their property investment loans more frequently than homeowners. That’s why lenders apply stricter underwriting standards to investors—and charge them a higher rate of interest and apply lower LTVs. Lenders would like to feel confident that you know what you are doing—and you’re not getting in over your head. Many investors today face foreclosure not only because they paid too much (relative to a property’s reasonably expected cash flows), but because they did not understand that rental properties (except for NNN properties) require management know-how.

To establish experience and competency, submit a business plan (of sorts) along with your loan application. Illustrate the managerial changes that you will execute to increase the property’s cash flows and boost its value. Convince the lender that you understand the market and can price and position your rentals to attract a well-defined target market of tenants who will prefer the units that you offer relative to those of your competition. Share with the lender your well-thought-out market strategy (see Chapter 11 and the example of Craig Wilson).

If you lack experience with property, relate other practical experiences that might seem similar. Or show the lender how you have succeeded in other ventures—that you have consistently performed your responsibilities diligently and competently. Show the lender that you are serious about long-term investing. You will bring the lender repeat business—and you will honor your commitments such that the lender will strive to retain you as a valued customer.





Compensating Factors


As you learn a lender’s underwriting guidelines, remember, these are guidelines. Most lenders do not evaluate their mortgage loan applications by inflexible rules. Lenders weigh and consider. You can persuade a lender to approve your loan. Emphasize your positives and play down or explain away negatives.

If your debt coverage ratio falls below a lender’s desired minimum, show the lender how you plan to improve the property and increase rents. If you have accumulated credit problems, compensate with a higher down payment or pledged collateral. Explain past foolishness or adverse circumstances. Show the new foundation for sound finances that you have laid. If you’ve frequently changed jobs, point out the raises and promotions you’ve received. If you lack experience in property ownership or property management, tell the lenders how you’ve educated yourself by reading real estate books and how you’ve developed a winning market strategy for your property.

Use employer letters, references, prepared budgets, a business plan, or any other written evidence that will increase the lender’s confidence that you will succeed with your property. Compensating factors can make the decision swing in your favor. Lenders differ—especially when they underwrite investors. What one rejects, another accepts. To obtain the loan (and loan amount) you want, search the mortgage market. With thousands of competing lenders and loan brokers, chances are you can find a lender (or seller) who’s right for you. If not, those lenders have just sent you a powerful signal: you must improve your financial fitness and borrower profile—your 7 Cs.





Automated Underwriting (AUS)


For the past 20 years, mortgage lenders have relied on automated underwriting. Using such a system, a loan rep gathers your pertinent underwriting facts and enters them into a software program.

If your borrower (and property) profile aligns with the standards written into this loan-qualifying program, great. It likely means a faster, less cumbersome path to closing, and a shorter stack of paperwork. But if your profile needs outside-the-box attention, work with a savvy loan rep who can apply the skill and knowledge necessary to get your loan approved—or at least tell you the reasons why your application falls short and how you can work to overcome deficiencies.

Automated underwriting (AUS) looks at more than your credit score. These programs incorporate calculations that evaluate your qualifying ratios, earning power, cash reserves, debts, and assets. When your credit scores exceed, say, 720 (or so), the AUS might relax on other standards. Whereas a score of, say, 640 (or so) will cause the AUS to subject your financial profile to stricter standards and more documentation of income, savings, and other pertinent financial data, though today, even 800-credit-score borrowers can expect excessive and often silly document requests and verifications.

An automated system never firmly approves or declines a loan. If your information registers a reject, you can still try to override the system with compensating factors and perhaps a direct appeal to the underwriting department or management higher-ups. Similarly, a looks-great feedback from AUS does not necessarily mean clear sailing. Underwriters—especially in tight credit markets—pore over every detail of your application and may ask you to further document anything from your grandmother’s maiden name to your dog’s birthday. Well, maybe not quite that demanding, but a green eyeshade underwriter can and will look to dot every i and cross every t. Today, rules rule over common sense. Prepare, persevere, and pray. The odds are good that your loan will eventually close.

1The term creative finance eludes precise definition. In general, it refers to the use of multiple sources of credit (e.g., sellers, real estate agents, contractors, partners) and out-of-the-norm financing techniques such as mortgage assumptions, subject-to purchases, land contracts, lease options, second or third mortgages, credit card cash advances, master leases, and so forth. In some circumstances, investors can use creative financing to buy real estate even though they lack cash or good credit. Each of these topics is discussed in this chapter.

2 Obviously, in high-priced areas of the country, $100,000 won’t buy a studio apartment. But to illustrate, it’s an easy figure to work with.

3From the late 1990s to 2006, some lenders offered investor loans with total LTVs as high as 100 percent. When the property boom receded, such liberal lenders either went broke because of excessive borrower defaults or tightened their qualifying to match or exceed more conservative (safer) underwriting standards.

4Many of these programs are described with applications in my book The 106 Mortgage Secrets that Borrowers Must Learn—But Lenders Don’t Tell, 2nd ed. (John Wiley & Sons, 2008).

5When a veteran makes a down payment, these loan amounts may be higher. Also, higher limits may apply in selected high-cost areas. The VA periodically increases its limits.

6In this case, the seller second served primarily to reduce my overall cost of borrowing thanks to its low interest rate.

7For a more extensive discussion of financing properties, see my book The 106 Mortgage Secrets that All Homebuyers Must Learn—but Lenders Don’t Tell, 2nd ed. (John Wiley & Sons, 2008).





Chapter 3

APPRAISAL: INS AND OUTS OF MARKET VALUE




Market value remains a centrally important metric (measure) to real estate investors, mortgage lenders, and homebuyers. Only the foolish would buy, sell, or loan against a property without knowledge of its market value. Nevertheless, hundreds of bank failures and millions of foreclosures has laid bare the idea that this popularly relied upon figure should serve as the only figure that matters.

Never again should anyone believe the once-entrenched view, “Buy for less than market value, you’ve scored a good deal. Pay more than market value, you’ve overpaid.”

Never again should anyone rely upon appraisers (without question) to provide accurate, disinterested estimates of market value. Although always known among real estate insiders, the market value estimates of appraisers are not only subject to unintentional errors of fact, interpretation, or both, in many instances, appraisers manipulate their numbers to deliver whatever value estimate makes a deal work (at least, that is, appear to work—in the short run.)

So, this chapter will help you in two ways:

You will understand how to review and critique a professionally prepared market value appraisal; and, correspondingly, you will wisely decide how much confidence you should place in the appraiser’s estimate of value.

You will see that for purposes of investment, even an accurately prepared market value appraisal fails to provide a figure that offers a fair or reasonable price to pay. For that guide, forecast future demand and supply, not rely on past sales prices.





WHAT IS MARKET VALUE?


To the naive, appraised value, sales price, and market value all refer to the same idea. But, actually, appraised value might refer to an insurance policy appraisal, a property tax appraisal, an estate tax appraisal, or a market value appraisal. Sales price itself reveals the nominal price at which a property has sold. That sales price might equal, exceed, or fall below market value. Sales price represents market value only when a property is sold according to these five assumptions:

Buyers and sellers are typically motivated. Neither acts under duress.

Buyers and sellers are well informed about the market and negotiate in their own best interest.

The marketing period and sales promotion bring the property to the attention of willing and able buyers.

No atypically favorable or unfavorable terms of financing apply. Easy money inflates demand. Tight money suppresses demand. (During the most recent property boom, lenders offered dangerously easy financing, thus pushing demand and sales prices far above the market values that would have prevailed under normal loan underwriting standards.)

Neither the sellers nor the buyers offer any extraordinary sales concessions or incentives. (For example, the builders in many countries offered off-plan buyers three years of rent guarantees—clearly a red flag that the builders’ prices exceed market value.)



To illustrate the assumed conditions underlying the concept of market value, say that two properties recently sold in a neighborhood where you might like to invest:

The house at 37 Oak sold at a price of $258,000, and 164 Maple sold at a price of $255,000. Each of these three-bedroom, two-bath houses was in good condition, with around 2,100 square feet. You locate a nearby house of similar size and features at 158 Pine. It’s priced at $234,750. Is that a below-market price? Maybe, maybe not. Before you accept this sales evidence, investigate the terms and conditions of the other two comparable sales.

What if the sellers of 164 Maple had carried back a nothing-down, 4 percent, 30-year mortgage for their buyers (that is, favorable financing)?

What if the buyers of 37 Oak had just flown into Peoria from San Francisco and bought the first house they saw because “It was such a steal. You couldn’t find anything like it in San Francisco for less than $1.2 million” (that is, uninformed buyers)?

What if the sellers of 37 Oak had agreed to pay all of their buyer’s closing costs and leave their authentic Chippendale buffet because it was too big to move into their new condo in Florida (that is, extraordinary sales concessions)? What if 37 Oak were a bank REO?





Sales Price Doesn’t Necessarily Equal Market Value


When you value property, learn more than the past prices at which so-called similar properties have recently sold. Investigate whether the buyers or sellers in these transactions acted with full market knowledge, negotiated any favorable terms of financing, bought (or sold) in a hurry, or conceded something that pushed up the nominal selling price—or perhaps pulled it down. If you find that the sales of comparable properties do not meet the conditions of a market value sale, then that sales price (unadjusted) does not necessarily imply a market value price.

To confidently rely on comp sales prices: verify the accuracy of your information, verify the date of sale, and verify the terms and conditions of the sale. Faulty information about a comp property’s features or terms of sale can make overpriced deals look good (or vice versa). Market value assumes no hidden defects or title issues. A comp (or subject) house with a termite infestation or unpaid tax liens should sell at a price less than market value (see later discussion).





Underwriting Rules Determine the Value in LTV


Banks loan against market value, not purchase price, unless your purchase price falls below market value. When you apply for a mortgage, you may tell the lender that you’ve agreed to a price of $200,000 and would like to borrow $160,000 (an 80 percent LTV). Yet the lender will not necessarily agree that this price matches the property’s market value.

The lender will ask about special financing terms (for example, a $20,000 seller second) and sales concessions (for example, the seller’s plan to buy down your interest rate for three years and pay all closing costs). If your transaction differs from market norms, the lender won’t lend 80 percent of your $200,000 purchase price—even if it routinely does make 80 percent LTV loans. The lender may find that easy terms of financing or sales concessions are worth $10,000. So, the lender may calculate your 80 percent LTV ratio against $190,000, not the $200,000 nominal purchase price.

To verify that your purchase price of $200,000 equals or exceeds market value, the lender will order a market value appraisal. If that appraisal report comes back with a figure that’s less than $200,000, the lender will use the lesser amount to calculate an 80 percent LTV loan. Take notice: Do not passively accept the results of any low (or high) appraisal. Review and critique the report. Ask the appraiser to correct errors. Or you can ask the lender to order a new appraisal with another firm. The lender needs a file document (appraisal) to justify its lending decision. If you provide an acceptable (revised or remade) appraisal of a satisfactory amount, you’ll often get the loan you want.

Danger: Just because a lender’s appraiser comes up with a market value estimate that matches your purchase price, never assume that the appraisal accurately sets market value. Accept personal responsibility for your offering price. In the past, loan reps routinely told their appraisers the value estimate they needed to make a deal work. In return, appraisers know that if they fail to hit the desired numbers, loan reps will select another, more accommodating appraiser to prepare their reports.

If you’re a good customer of a bank (or if the bank would like you to become a good customer), the loan rep may encourage the appraiser to issue an MAI (made as instructed) appraisal. I know of many instances in which appraisers have acquiesced to not-so-subtle hints from a loan rep and submitted appraisals that overstated a property’s value. (Indeed, as early in the property boom as 2003, government investigators found that loan reps were pressing appraisers to lift their value estimates.)

New lender and appraisal regulations supposedly will stop this abuse. I would not bet on it. People in the business know each other—and word gets around. There’s no Chinese Wall in real estate. Recently, a property that I own was reappraised so that I could refinance and pull cash out (to reinvest, not spend) some of that almost free money (3.75 percent interest rate). When inspecting the house, the appraiser asked me what I thought it was worth. His market value estimate came in a mere $5,000 less than the number I suggested. In this instance, my estimate was accurate. I am not accusing the appraiser of over- or undervaluing. But it does illustrate that he did not want to nix the loan with an out-of-range opinion.

You will work with appraisers, and you will solicit their opinions, but never accept those opinions as the final word. To protect yourself against inaccurate appraisals (your own, as well as others), understand how to calculate, apply, and interpret the three basic methods used to estimate market value.





HOW TO ESTIMATE MARKET VALUE


To estimate market value, rely on multiple methods. During the property and credit boom, appraisal practice focused on the comparable sales approach and ignored (or slighted) the cost and income approaches. In doing so, appraisers missed the danger signals that the cost and income methods were flashing.

Cost approach Calculate how much it would cost to build a subject property at today’s prices.

Subtract accrued depreciation.

Add the depreciated cost figure to the current value of the lot.





Comparable sales approach Compare a subject property with other similar (comp) properties that have recently sold.

Adjust the prices for each positive or negative feature of the comps relative to the subject property.

Estimate market value of the subject property from the adjusted sales prices of the comps.





Income approach Estimate the rents you expect a property to produce.

Convert net rents after expenses (net operating income) into a capital (market) value amount. Alternatively, estimate the gross amount of rents that a property could bring in and multiply that amount by an applicable market-derived GRM.





When you evaluate a property from three perspectives, you check the value estimates of each against the others. Multiple estimates and techniques enhance the probability that your estimate reflects reality. If your three value estimates don’t reasonably match up, either your calculations err, the figures you’re working with are inaccurate, or the market is acting crazy and property prices are about to head up (or down).

Figure 3.1 shows a sample residential appraisal form for a single-family house. Refer to this form as you read the following pages and you’ll see how to apply these three techniques to appraise properties. Photocopy this form (or print a copy from the Internet). Use the forms to fill in property and market information as you value potential property investments.





FIGURE 3.1 Appraisal Report





PROPERTY DESCRIPTION


To accurately estimate the market value of a property, first describe the features of the property and its neighborhood in detail. List all facts that might influence value favorably or unfavorably. Investors err in their appraisals because they casually inspect rather than carefully detail and compare. Focus on each of the neighborhood and property features listed on an appraisal form. You will judge properties more profitably. (Here I focus on market value inspection and description. Valuing for investment and entrepreneurial improvements differs in perspective and purpose. We address those perspectives in following chapters.)





Identify the Subject Property


To identify the subject property seems straightforward. But all is not as simple as you may think. The street address for one of my previous homes was 73 Roble Road, Berkeley, California 94705. However, that property does not sit in Berkeley. It is actually located in Oakland. The house sat back from Roble Road (which is in Berkeley) about 100 feet—just far enough to place it within the city limits of Oakland. As a result, the city laws governing the property (zoning, building regulations, permits, rent controls, school district, and so forth) were those of Oakland, not Berkeley.

Similarly, Park Cities (University Park and Highland Park) are high- income, independent municipalities located within the geographic boundaries of Dallas, Texas. Among other amenities, Park Cities are noted for their high-quality schools. Yet (in the past) if you lived in Park Cities on the west side of the North Dallas Tollway, your children would attend the lesser-regarded schools of the Dallas Independent School District.

The lesson: Street and city addresses don’t tell you what you need to know about a property. Strange as it may seem, a property may not be located where you think it is. The property may not receive the services you think it does. The laws of zoning and other regulations may not apply as you think they do. (See also the discussion of site identification further on.)





Neighborhood


As the appraisal form shows, a neighborhood investigation should note the types and condition of neighborhood properties, the percentage of houses and condominiums that are owner occupied, vacancy rates, property price (and rental) ranges, the types and quality of government services, and the relative convenience of the property to shopping, schools, employment centers, and parks and recreational areas—the appeal of the neighborhood to potential buyers.

Next, bring the future into view. Envision the changes that are likely to occur in the neighborhood during the coming three to five years. Is the neighborhood stable? Is it moving toward higher rates of owner occupancy? Are property owners fixing up their properties? Do neighborhood residents and local merchants take pride in their properties and the surrounding area? Is a neighborhood (or homeowners’) association working to improve the area? If not, could such an association make the neighborhood a better place to live, shop, work, and play?

Have builders added large numbers of new housing units to the area? Is the neighborhood filled with foreclosures, For Sale signs or For Rent signs, or both? Has excess supply driven down property prices? How many months (or years) might be needed to clear existing and pipeline inventories?

Investment value looks to the future. Market value chiefly looks to past sales of similar properties. When you invest, you buy the future more than the present. View the neighborhood with a magnifying glass and with the use of a crystal ball. How will (or could) the neighborhood appear, look, feel, and be made to live in within the coming 5 to 10 years?





Site (Lot) Characteristics


Depending on the neighborhood, the size and features of a lot can account for 20 to 80 percent of a property’s current and future value. Smart investors pay as much attention to the lot (and its potential) as they do to the building(s).

In addition to site size and features (see appraisal form), review the rules and restrictions that govern a site. Determine whether the buildings conform to zoning, occupancy, environmental, and safety regulations. Many two- to four-unit (and larger) properties have been modified (rehabbed, cut up, added to, repaired, renovated, rewired, reroofed, and so on) in ways that violate current law. Laws also change.
