The remaining term of the lease, option-to-renew clauses, above- or below-market lease rates, the quality of the property’s location, property appreciation potential, and the cost and availability of property financing also influence the cap rate investors apply to a stream of NNN income.

Favorable deal issues tend to drive cap rates lower, and unfavorable issues tend to drive cap rates higher. No single rule applies as to the right cap rate or the best deal. For example, a Walgreens property priced with a 5.5 percent cap rate might prove to offer more long-term profit potential than a mom and pop–operated, locally owned sushi restaurant priced with a 12 percent cap rate. Before deciding, go through your due diligence checklist for each property that you consider. Then relative to the pros and cons, choose the one that seems to fit your risk-and-reward goals best. To assist in deriving the appropriate cap rate (and corresponding pricing) for any given deal, experienced and beginning investors solicit advice from an experienced, active NNN real estate sales agent.

Most triple net deals offer little or no possibility for entrepreneurial property improvement during the period of the lease (though exceptions exist), but they do offer the ultimate in carefree property investing—yet still provide returns from cash flows, appreciation, amortization, leverage, tax shelter, and inflation. As more boomers near their retirement years, I expect increasing demand for NNN properties. In a point-by-point comparison to stocks, CDs, annuities, or bonds, NNN properties—on a risk-adjusted basis—often prove superior.

Which sounds better to you: a 10-year U.S. government Treasury note that yields 1.92 percent, a 5-year bank CD that yields 1.67 percent, a portfolio of S&P 500 stocks that yield 1.9 percent, gold that yields 0.0 percent, a Walgreen’s 20-year, NNN property yielding 6.3 percent with modest rent bumps every 5 years—or maybe a Dollar General (corporate guarantee) currently yielding 8.1 percent on 15-year NNN lease? (All actual yields at the time this chapter was written in January, 2012.)





SELF-STORAGE


Americans have become a population of pack rats. Their want of storage space grows larger every year. As a result, the self-storage industry has continued to grow. Although the big national players who operate 200- to 400-unit facilities attract most of the self-storage business, smaller investors, too, can profit with smaller (and sometimes more specialized) properties. Self-storage can offer cap rates of 8 to 12 percent. Earn even more if the site will accommodate expansion. Alternatively, self-storage can provide a profitable way to hold land while you wait, say 5 to 10 years, until the site becomes suitable for redevelopment into a more valuable use (for example, retail, offices).

In today’s market, self-storage is difficult to bank finance unless you place 30 percent (or more) down and show evidence that your prior business experience equips you to competently manage the premises. This tight financing presents opportunity and danger.

Opportunity because motivated sellers recognize that they will need to offer OWC with favorable terms if they want to maximize the sales potential of their property. (The same situation applies to small mobile home parks; see the next section.) Danger because OWC can entice the unwary into both overpaying—as well as underestimating the business skill necessary to turn self-storage into a cash cow.

With rental rates per square foot that can almost match the rental rates of mid-range apartments, self-storage certainly looks like a no-brainer. No midnight calls about stopped-up toilets, no loud parties that command the attention of the police, little or no worries about tenants soiling the carpets or stuffing steak bones down the garbage disposal.

Although self-storage avoids problems that inept owners of residential rentals suffer, it does present some potential difficulties that only the inexperienced ignore. Here are a few:

Customer turnover. By its very nature, self-storage appeals to transient customers. Effective retention and replacement reign supreme. To retain good-paying customers requires selection and service. When vacancies occur, you must quickly fill the space. Each of these tasks calls for something more than a night watchman attitude. You need marketing savvy.

Poor location. To succeed typically requires a highly visible location with a traffic count of 25,000 vehicles a day. You also need effective signage and easy and convenient egress and ingress. If your property lacks any of these essentials, you had better develop a very good Plan B.

Rent collection procedures. The self-storage industry persistently looks for policies and procedures that will get rents paid on time, every time. Otherwise, that cash cow you were hoping to find soon runs dry. Auctioning off contents does not substitute for rent collection.

Lien laws. If you must auction contents, follow your state’s lien laws precisely. Failure to abide by governing rules will subject owners to fines and lawsuits.

Demographics matter. Your customers will consist primarily of middle class and perhaps some businesses (depending on your market strategy). Typically, industry experience shows that the lower-income households lack both the excess possessions that end up in self-storage and the discretionary income necessary to pay the rent. Since customers tend to select storage units by convenience, verify whether the neighborhood demographics and population density can satisfy your need for profitability.

Verify owner financials. As with mobile home parks, smaller owner-operators of self-storage properties rarely provide potential buyers an accurate and comprehensive statement of current and past years’ income and expenses. To protect yourself, check and crosscheck all figures: tax records, utility companies, talks with customers (if possible), a review of leases and rental applications (if used). Seek explicit representations and warranties when negotiations give you the leverage to do so. Accept nothing on faith.



Ineptly managed, self-storage can drain your cash faster than it produces it. That’s why banks have wised up from their anyone-qualifies underwriting and now want to see a business plan backed up by managerial know-how. Banks also understand—as do experienced operators—that self-storage can provide some of the highest returns available to property investors. But to earn those generous cash flows—and contrary to popular perception—the self-storage business does not run well on automatic pilot. As with most types of actively managed real estate, you—as much as, or even more than, the property itself—will bring in those good cash flows.





MOBILE HOME PARKS


Mobile home parks (MHPs) yield high cash flows—if you buy right. As with self-storage, many property investors are lured into the potential high returns of MHPs without performing the requisite competitive market analysis and property due diligence. And to an even greater degree than self-storage, sellers of MHPs entice the naïve with OWC financing and cooked books to whet the appetite.

What should your due diligence include? Here are some essentials, but prior to buying, dig much deeper into the many how-to MHP guides.

Owner financials. Verify all income and expense figures through multiple checking and cross-checking. Compare to industry norms. Calculate annual gross income and annual gross expenses on a monthly per-space basis. Now do the figures look reasonable? Obtain park bank statements, tax returns, and operating income history for the past three years. The more reluctant the owner is to provide good financial records, the more risk you confront.

Zoning. Zoning boards have become hostile to mobile home parks in many areas of the country. In one sense, this trend works for you because it limits new competition. But it can work against you if your park now fails to meet current zoning (or other land use) rules and regulations. Perhaps the number of park spaces exceeds the number permitted. Some zoning laws grandfather existing nonconforming uses, but only for a limited number of years.

Rent rolls. Review the lease and personal record for all tenants who currently reside in the park as well as rent roll records for the past two or three years. Current and past tenants can provide insight about park operations. Plus, learn their source of income, rent-paying history, and length of tenure in the park—and if you can, why previous tenants moved.

Sewer/Water lines. Many mobile home parks lack public sewer and water connections. This deficiency opens up multiple health and environmental issues. Capacity, pressure, pipe quality, size, age, and condition raise other related concerns. A professional inspection and perhaps a Phase 1 environmental report can alert you to existing and potential problems. Even if park lines are connected to city facilities, inspect them. City responsibility often ends at the property border. Repairing or replacing the park’s underground piping can cost many thousands of dollars.

Police reports. What do the police or sheriff’s department think of the park and its surrounding area? Are officers called frequently to investigate crimes or disturbances?

Walk the grounds. Walk, talk, inspect, discover. What ambience pervades the park? What demographic dominates? Do residents abide by park rules? Do park rules sufficiently control barking dogs, trash disposal, loud or offensive behavior, parking, discarded appliances and other junk and debris, inoperable vehicles, improperly muffled vehicles? Would the park appeal to the type of resident that you would like to attract?

Substitute housing. If the residents did not live here, where else could they live? Are competing parks nearby? What are lot rental rates? What amounts do rental apartments and rental houses charge? How well can this park compete with the substitutes: surroundings, accessibility, attractiveness, price?



“Any idiot can buy a mobile home park, and many do,” writes an old pro who has been in the business for more than 25 years. He continues, “Many, many people have made themselves millionaires through owning mobile home parks. In Stanley and Danko’s book, The Millionaire Next Door, owning mobile home parks ranked as one of the top routes to build such wealth. However, these folks knew what they were doing. They learned the business, performed proper due diligence, and operated professionally.”

To become a millionaire next door, you can invest in mobile home parks in two ways:

Own the pads, provide utilities, and maintain the site.

Own the pads and the mobile homes (much more labor intensive for maintenance and tenant turnover).



As with self-storage, you can hold mobile home parks for cash flow until a higher and better use for the land becomes profitable. It’s also customary to delegate some day-to-day management to a park tenant who would like to earn extra money.

If you buy a park that includes mobile home rentals (rather than just the pads), value the land separately from the trailers. First, capitalize the lot rental NOI. Add to that figure the depreciated market value of the trailers. Rental mobile homes generate strong cash flows. But they lose value quickly. If you capitalize their rental income to figure a market price, you will overpay.

You can easily buy repossessed mobile homes for a fraction of their cost new and at a fraction of their capitalized value. That’s why mobile homes generate so much cash flow per dollar invested. In most markets, a mobile home costing $10,000 (or less) will rent for $450 (more or less). A brick-and-mortar apartment building bringing in about the same amount of rental revenue could command a price of $30,000 to $40,000 per unit.





PROFITABLE POSSIBILITIES WITH ZONING


Research whether you can upgrade a property’s use and profitability within current zoning and regulatory rules. Sometimes zoning laws change and the affected property owners don’t notice—or they just don’t investigate the more profitable uses that the rules now permit. Alternatively, persuade the planners and elected officials to grant your property (or area) a higher and better use. (This tactic is discussed in Chapter 9, but I wish to remind you here.)

As property investors struggle to boost their yields, zoning and land use opportunities are becoming more important. If you can add another floor to a rental house, sell off extra land as a buildable lot, or convert a house to an adult congregate living facility (ACLF), you can pocket thousands of dollars of found money. (For more details on the types of changes you might seek, see pages 54 to 88 in my book Make Money with Flippers, Fixer-Uppers, and Renovations 2nd ed. [John Wiley & Sons, 2008]. To help you navigate the approval process, see The Complete Guide to Zoning by Dwight Merriam [McGraw-Hill, 2005].)





TAX LIENS AND TAX DEEDS


Throughout the United States, nearly every state and county levies taxes against properties. If an owner fails to pay this tax assessment, the county authorities (sooner or later) begin to enforce their claim. Generally, this enforcement involves a tax lien, a tax deed, or some combination of both.





Localities Differ


Each jurisdiction proceeds according to local and state law. In my county in Florida, the assessor’s office sells tax liens to investors in a reverse-bid auction. The bid winner pays the tax of the defaulting property owner. That investor then earns interest on his tax lien of 18 percent a year or less—in recent years much less.

The county starts the bid at 18 percent. Then investors bid down the rate. The lowest-rate bidder wins. In an auction that I recently attended, most tax liens were sold at an interest rate of less than 7 percent.

If the property owner does not repay the investor, including interest accrued, within four years, the investor can petition the court to order a tax sale auction. The investor who bids the highest purchase price at the tax deed sale (who may or may not be the tax lien investor) is then issued a tax deed to the property. The tax lien investor is paid from the tax sale proceeds. If the auction’s sales proceeds do not cover the full amount of the tax lien (with interest), tough luck for the tax lien investor.

Upon being awarded a tax deed, the tax deed investor then employs a lawyer at a cost of $2,000 (more or less) to try to clear the legal issues that will cloud the title. (Tax deeds rarely qualify for title insurance.) If successful, the tax deed investor then moves to the next stage of his strategy (sell, renovate, rent out, whatever).





Are Tax Liens and Tax Deeds an Easy Way to Make Big Profits?


If you believe that you can easily earn big profits through tax liens or tax deeds, you’ve been watching too many infomercials. You can earn profits in this arena only if you work hard to research markets, research properties, and research legal procedures. Then bid selectively when your research indicates a satisfactory trade-off between risk and reward. For a realistic view of tax liens and tax deeds and a state-by-state listing of legal procedures, see Profit by Investing in Real Estate Tax Liens by Larry Loftis (Dearborn, 2005).





DISCOUNTED PAPER


Discounted paper represents another investment technique famously promoted by the moguls of mentoring, boot camp gurus, and infomercial pitchmen. In fact, one such trainer was put out of business by the U.S. Department of Justice and is serving time in federal prison. Does this clampdown mean that discounted paper itself offers no opportunities to individual investors? No. But it does indicate that some of the gurus of discounted paper—like their compatriots who infest other fields of real estate gurudom—overpromise, underdeliver, and overcharge.





What Is Discounted Paper?


To entice a buyer, property sellers sometimes carry back financing, which investors refer to as paper (for example, “I got a good deal on that property. The seller agreed carry back paper for me.”) If at some later date, that OWC seller wants to convert his buyer’s paper (a mortgage or contract-for-deed) to ready cash, he assigns the buyer’s promise to pay and property lien to a note investor. How do note sellers and buyers find each other? Postings on the Internet, hundreds of note brokers, and newspaper classified ads represent the primary avenues of contact.

How much does the note investor pay for the right to receive the loan payments of the buyer or debtor? That amount is negotiated between the seller and the investor. The current outstanding balance on the loan usually sets the top price, but the note investor typically pays the seller something less (that is, a loan balance of $145,000 might sell for a price of $122,000.) On occasion, though, in low-interest-rate times like the present, some note investors might be willing to pay a premium over the outstanding loan balance—just as bond investors will pay a price premium over a bond’s face value, if the bond yield exceeds the current market interest rate (for bonds of the same credit quality).





Here’s How the Loan Sale Works


A seller carries back a 7 percent, $100,000 second mortgage, 25-year amortization. Based on those terms, the buyer pays the seller $707 a month. After three years, the seller needs cash. At that time, the remaining balance on the buyer’s note equals $95,100. If a note investor wants to earn 12 percent on his money, he would pay the seller $65,588—which equals the present value of $707 per month for 22 years (the remaining term of the loan) discounted at 12 percent. The buyer then makes his monthly payments to the note buyer. At the reduced purchase price, the loan now yields an effective interest rate of 12 percent.





Sell the Note at a Premium


Assume that you purchased this loan two years ago, but you are not keen to hold this debtor’s note for 20 years. You want fast money, and fortunately for you, market interest rates have fallen. In such a situation, you could search for a note investor who will accept a 10 percent return. For the right to receive $707 per month for 20 years, this investor would pay $73,353. Flip the note to this investor and pocket $7,765 more than the $65,588 you originally paid. The new note investor enjoys an income stream of $8,484 each year for 20 years, which includes a return of his $73,353 original investment plus an annual return on his investment of 10 percent.

Not many people know of this private paper market—even though it operates in principle just as the banks and mortgage companies buy and sell mortgages among themselves, and among mortgage investors such as pension funds and life insurance companies. Given the need for income among the 3 to 4 million people who retire every year, and given the small-to-miniscule yields offered by bonds, stocks, and CDs, I expect the demand for private paper to grow. And given the fact that most outstanding paper has been written at interest rates of 6 to 12 percent, buying at a discount and selling at a premium might become another important way to earn good returns.





Delinquent and Nonperforming Loans


Want to buy a loan at a 40 percent, 50 percent, or maybe even a 75 percent discount? Step up your risk tolerance and enter the field of buying paper that has gone bad. Although you may never collect from the debtor, that paper provides you a lien against the collateral property. If loan workout efforts with the debtor fail, you could ask him for a deed-in-lieu of foreclosure or proceed to a foreclosure sale.

Some investors accept a deed-in-lieu and then permit the homeowners to remain in the house under a lease or lease option. If you choose to take the property to a foreclosure sale you can bid up to whatever amount sets the minimum price you want. If another bidder goes higher, you’re out with a nice profit. If no one outbids you, you now own the property for whatever price you paid for the discounted loan plus foreclosure costs.

If state law permits a deficiency judgment, you could go after the property owner for additional monies that he is legally obligated to pay under the original loan agreement. You pay $50,000 for a loan that shows a $100,000 balance. You let the property sell at foreclosure to another bidder for $60,000. You have earned a profit, but because the note balance is $100,000, the debtor still owes you the difference between the outstanding loan balance (plus legal costs, late fees, and accrued interest) and the $60,000 you obtained from the foreclosure sale.

Can you collect? Probably not, if the debtor files bankruptcy—and the court permits a complete discharge. But if the debtor appears able to pay at least part of your claim (and the claims of other creditors), the law does not authorize a complete wipeout of all debts. The court will order partial payment. If the debtor does not file bankruptcy, your judgment will remain in the records (depending on the statute of limitations) for 10 to 20 years (or longer.)

When I mentioned short sales (p. 131), I said you need the cooperation of the property owner or the deal would go nowhere. Technically, that’s right. But you can try another approach. Offer to buy the seller’s delinquent loan from the bank. With loan in hand, you can proceed to recovery of your investment in any of the ways that we’ve just discussed.

With a short sale, the lender will permit you to arrange a loan with another bank to pay off the defaulted loan. If you purchase the debt, you will probably need to raise the cash to do so. Still, for investors who have or can raise the cash, buying nonperforming loans has become big business. In fact, numerous vulture funds have raised hundreds of millions of dollars to buy packages of defaulted mortgage debt. (Similarly, companies buy nonperforming car loans and credit card accounts.)





Due Diligence Issues


The discount loan market requires homework. What’s the true market value of the mortgaged property? (The seller and original mortgagee/note holder may have jacked up the selling price of his property and sucked in a naïve buyer with an offer of easy financing.) What credit risk does the borrower present? What’s the debtor’s payment record to date? What’s the quality of the property’s title? Is the debtor current on all senior liens (that is, the first mortgage, property taxes, HOA assessments, federal income taxes)?

Do the mortgage agreement and note comply with applicable state and federal laws? Did the seller originate the loan with any and all required disclosures and a truth-in-lending statement? If the buyer defaults, what procedures must the note holder follow to foreclose? How long will it take? How much will it cost? Can the note holder collect a deficiency judgment? If so, under what circumstances?

No doubt about it. You can earn good money buying or brokering discounted paper. Just verify that the paper you buy is worth substantially more than the paper itself—which is not always the case.





SHOULD YOU FORM AN LLC?


Here’s another concern that the gurus have popularized during the past several years: “Don’t build your wealth only to lose it to a lawsuit or judgment creditor. Protect your assets through limited liability companies (LLCs).” In response to such publicity and promotion, many of my readers have asked me, “Should we hold our property (or properties) in LLC(s)?”





Different Strokes for Different Folks


Neither I nor anyone else can answer that question for you until we study your legal, financial, and tax situation in light of your goals and what you want your LLC(s) to accomplish. Many mortgage lenders will not permit you to borrow against a property that’s held by an LLC, nor will they permit you to transfer a property to an LLC after you have closed your loan. Holding a property in an LLC might also bear upon income tax and estate-planning issues.





Court Rulings


To what degree will an LLC protect your assets against lawsuits or creditors? Those remain open questions. They have not yet been litigated enough to establish a clear body of case law. Moreover, LLC statutes are enacted by states, not Congress. What proves true for Texas may not protect in the same way for New York or Indiana.

Even within the same state, the different district courts of appeals may rule inconsistently with one another. Until (if ever) the high court of the state clarifies or overrules the LLC law in, say, Tampa (2nd DCA), the law could differ from the LLC law in Orlando (5th DCA) even though both courts presumably apply the same Florida statute.





One Size Doesn’t Fit All


You need asset protection. LLCs represent one potential means to achieve it. Whether it’s the best means for your situation, no one knows until they review your financial, tax, and legal situation; each of the asset protection alternatives that are available to you; and the applicable law. In contrast to the hype surrounding LLCs, one size doesn’t fit all.

Please do not misunderstand. You need asset protection. But look further than off-the-shelf LLC solutions. Your circumstances and state laws will control what works best for you. This is one area for which you need tailored, expert legal advice. Remember, too, liability insurance plays a necessary part of any comprehensive asset protection program. Do not merely buy insurance. Read your policy perils, coverages, limits, and exclusions.

To guard against lawsuits and other calamities, insurance stands as your first and most important risk management technique. Even within LLCs, property and liability insurance provides the critical component of your wealth protection plan. (Of course, your property operating policies and practices actually represent your first line of preparation for loss prevention. Obey all laws, nurture good tenant relations, and maintain the premises in good repair.)





Chapter 16

OPPORTUNITY FOR A LIFETIME




Promoters of investment schemes often talk about “the opportunity of a lifetime. In fact, you’ve probably heard someone say, “If you do not buy now, if you pass up what I’m offering, you’ll miss the opportunity of a lifetime.”

Property investing differs. Property investing provides you an opportunity for a lifetime. Market timing matters, but building a storehouse of knowledge, abilities, and entrepreneurial talents matters more. As you gain experience, as you find your bearings, as you develop your market wisdom, you will consistently find—and, even better—create new opportunities. You will see that the deal of a lifetime actually comes along at least once a month—if you remain alert and educated enough to recognize it.

Throughout this book, I have illustrated multiple types of investment possibilities and explained how and why these multiple possibilities can yield multiple sources of wealth-building potential. The economists, financial planners, and assorted media commentators who think that to accurately compare property to stocks, one simply compares the relative gains in price between these two asset classes err egregiously. Fortunately for you and me, their error reduces our competition. And that error especially helps now.





USA: RIGHT TIME, RIGHT PRICE, RIGHT PLACE


Although property investing does not require market timing, market timing heightens the potential for scoring great deals. By great deal, I not only refer to good price and good property as related to today’s market prices. More importantly, today’s investors will look back from 5, 10, or 20 years into the future and congratulate themselves for acting while the majority waited in idle worry and procrastination. Looking back from the future, today’s opportunities will really stand out as great deals.

“Foreign capital has piled into apartment blocks and residential homes in the United States this past year. International investors seek a safe haven in America’s rental property.” This recent Financial Times article continues, “[Foreign] investors are seeking less risky assets whose values won’t be as volatile as other investments. . . . There are strong, long-term demand drivers and limited new supply coming into the market. Apartment rent growth will outpace the economic recovery.”

It seems that foreign investors have once again realized the potential for U.S. real estate. Record amounts of cash are pouring in from Canada, the United Kingdom, the Netherlands, and other nations of Europe, Asia, and the Middle East. Should not more Americans join with this thumbs-up endorsement of property investing? Low interest rates, high affordability, higher cap rates, lower GRMs, stable-to-decreasing vacancies, stable-to-increasing rents, prices below replacement costs, and a paucity of new construction—a combination of positive market indicators that remain unmatched by any other time in our living history.

No one knows or can know the future for Social Security, stock prices, bond returns, or inflation. Social Security must raise taxes and cut payouts or it will go broke. Stock prices, bond prices, interest rates, and inflation all show big, unexpected ups and downs. Examine the record for any of these and you see that uncertainty reigns.

We do know, however, that neither stocks nor bonds, and not even certificates of deposit provide an inflation-adjusted income stream that 80 million current and future retirees think sufficient or reliable. And who will act so foolishly as to count on Social Security to fund an adequate living standard and quality of life? Upon evaluating the alternatives, property investing wins by default. No other choice provides a reasonably sufficient income.

Yet, does property win only by default? I think not, but you can judge for yourself.





PERSONAL OPPORTUNITY


When you rely on stocks, bonds, or Social Security, your fate wanders outside of your control. Other than buying, selling, or perhaps voting (for Social Security increases), you can do nothing to influence the returns you would like to receive from these assets. Your financial goals mean nothing to stock markets, bond markets, or Congress. Not so with properties.

When you choose property as your opportunity for a lifetime, you can achieve good returns, even in a recession. (Or, as I would advise, especially in a recession. Because that’s when you can most easily pick up bargain-priced properties.) More importantly, investing in property provides profit sources that stock, bank certificates of deposit, or bond investors can never achieve. Recall the discussion of Chapter 1:

Buy properties at prices substantially less than their current market value.

Through creative finance, acquire properties with little of your own cash (but heed the caveats).

Accumulate large amounts of equity through amortization. Tenants pay off your loan and buy your properties for you.

Improve properties to enhance their market value.

Improve your market strategy to boost rents and lower vacancies.

Cut operating costs to increase net operating income.

Sell your existing properties and trade up without paying tax on your capital gains. Pocket your positive cash flows free of income taxes (up to the limit of your deduction for depreciation).

Change the use of a property from one that’s less profitable to one more profitable (apartments to condo, residential to office).

Refinance your properties and pull out tax-free cash. (You can borrow short-term against a stock portfolio. But margin requirements, price volatility, and little or no cash flow overload that choice with costs and risks.)



To profit from real estate requires due diligence, knowledge, effort, time, and market savvy (though NNN properties do offer a passive income stream with cash flows and capital growth that exceed alternatives). Unlike those who buy stocks, real property investors don’t whimsically follow hot tips from Internet chat rooms, barbers, hair stylists, auto mechanics, neighbors, or the chatter of talking heads on cable TV. You can’t buy, own, or sell real estate with the click of a mouse. (Of course, the last boom did attract many who assumed they could profitably speculate risk-free in property while guided by a stock gambling mentality.)

But the so-called disadvantages of work, research, and reason explain why, over a period of years, real estate provides a safer, surer route to lifetime income and a comfortably secure net worth. Today, fanciful dreamers still believe that they and tens of millions of other Americans can achieve wealth without work. Just buy stocks, and voilà—their portfolio will grow. Supposedly, easy fortune awaits all who faithfully pay into their 403(b)s, 401(k)s, Keoghs, 529s, and IRAs. But it won’t happen. It can’t happen.

At some time in the future, Americans will realize that the income they receive from their stock portfolios can never exceed the amount of corporate dividends. And these dividends will never grow large enough to support the more than 50 million hopefuls who continue to send their monthly retirement savings to Wall Street. (Talk about risky behavior!)

At present, income property provides true investment opportunities. You can speculate in stocks. You can speculate in real estate. You can buy lottery tickets. You can buy gold or trade in foreign exchange. You can shoot craps in Las Vegas. Maybe any or all of these wealth-without-work techniques will pay off, but the odds are stacked against you. In lieu of these popular choices, selectively accumulate 6 to 12 properties (residential or commercial), and you will receive income and build assets for life—a monthly cash flow that will finance the quality of life you would like to enjoy. You consume the fruits: the tree continues to grow.

I wish you success. And let me know how you’re progressing. I like to hear from readers and investors (gweldred@gmail.com).
